document.addEventListener('accessdetailsloaded', function(e) {
    var dL, doi, pageType, eventObject, i, script, loginEventData;

    for (i = 0; i < window.dataLayer.length; i++) {
        if (window.dataLayer[i]["content"] && window.dataLayer[i]["page"]) {
            dL = window.dataLayer[i];
        }
    }

    if (!dL
        || !dL.content.article
        || !(doi = dL.content.article.doi) && doi.length > 0
        || (pageType = dL.page.category.pageType.toLowerCase()) !== "article"
    ) {return false;}

    pageType = pageType.charAt(0).toUpperCase() + pageType.substring(1);

    eventObject = {'content_type': pageType, 'doi': doi};

    loginEventData = e.detail;
    if (loginEventData && loginEventData.business_partner_id) {
        eventObject['business_partner_ids'] = loginEventData.business_partner_id;
    }

    script = document.createElement('script');
    script.id = 'springerlink-event-tracker';
    script.src = 'https://event-tracker.springernature.com/dist/eventTracker.js';
    script.onload = function() {
        new EventTracker({'platform': 'Nature'}).sendEvent('display', eventObject);
    };
    document.head.appendChild(script);
});
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Network-based prediction of drug combinations"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-03-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Drug combinations, offering increased therapeutic efficacy and reduced toxicity, play an important role in treating multiple complex diseases. Yet, our ability to identify and validate effective combinations is limited by a combinatorial explosion, driven by both the large number of drug pairs as well as dosage combinations. Here we propose a network-based methodology to identify clinically efficacious drug combinations for specific diseases. By quantifying the network-based relationship between drug targets and disease proteins in the human protein&#8211;protein interactome, we show the existence of six distinct classes of drug&#8211;drug&#8211;disease combinations. Relying on approved drug combinations for hypertension and cancer, we find that only one of the six classes correlates with therapeutic effects: if the targets of the drugs both hit disease module, but target separate neighborhoods. This finding allows us to identify and validate antihypertensive combinations, offering a generic, powerful network methodology to identify efficacious combination therapies in drug development. Combination therapy holds great promise, but discovery remains challenging. Here, the authors propose a method to identify efficacious drug combinations for specific diseases, and find that successful combinations tend to target separate neighbourhoods of the disease module in the human interactome."/>

                            <h1 class="c-article-title u-h1" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Network-based prediction of drug combinations</h1>

                    <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                        <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="Abs1-content"><p>Drug combinations, offering increased therapeutic efficacy and reduced toxicity, play an important role in treating multiple complex diseases. Yet, our ability to identify and validate effective combinations is limited by a combinatorial explosion, driven by both the large number of drug pairs as well as dosage combinations. Here we propose a network-based methodology to identify clinically efficacious drug combinations for specific diseases. By quantifying the network-based relationship between drug targets and disease proteins in the human protein–protein interactome, we show the existence of six distinct classes of drug–drug–disease combinations. Relying on approved drug combinations for hypertension and cancer, we find that only one of the six classes correlates with therapeutic effects: if the targets of the drugs both hit disease module, but target separate neighborhoods. This finding allows us to identify and validate antihypertensive combinations, offering a generic, powerful network methodology to identify efficacious combination therapies in drug development.</p></div></div></section>

                        

                        
                            <section aria-labelledby="Sec1"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="Sec1-content"><p>Combination therapy, the use of multiple drugs to improve clinical outcomes, has multiple advantages compared to monotherapy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sun, X., Vilar, S. &amp; Tatonetti, N. P. High-throughput methods for combinatorial drug discovery. Sci. Transl. Med. 5, 205rv201 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4768e552">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Jia, J. et al. Mechanisms of drug combinations: interaction and network perspectives. Nat. Rev. Drug Discov. 8, 111–128 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4768e555">2</a></sup>: it offers higher efficacies or, through lower individual dosage, it can reduce the risk of adverse effects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ali, M. A., Rizvi, S. &amp; Syed, B. A. Trends in the market for antihypertensive drugs. Nat. Rev. Drug Discov. 16, 309–310 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4768e559">3</a></sup>. Consequently, combination therapies are widely used in the treatment of multiple complex diseases, from hypertension <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Giles, T. D. et al. Efficacy and safety of nebivolol and valsartan as fixed-dose combination in hypertension: a randomised, multicentre study. Lancet 383, 1889–1898 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4768e563">4</a></sup> to cancer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Crystal, A. S. et al. Patient-derived models of acquired resistance can identify effective drug combinations for cancer. Science 346, 1480–1486 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4768e567">5</a></sup> and infectious diseases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tan, X. et al. Systematic identification of synergistic drug pairs targeting HIV. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 1125–1130 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4768e571">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zheng, W., Sun, W., &amp; Simeonov, A. Drug repurposing screens and synergistic drug-combinations for infectious diseases. Br. J. Pharmacol. 175, 181–191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4768e574">7</a></sup>. However, the systematic identification of drug combinations that simultaneously offer high clinical efficacy and low toxicity is often driven by intuition and experience rather than established principles. There is a pressing need, therefore, for novel methodologies to facilitate the discovery of multicomponent therapy.</p><p>One approach is the systematic high-throughput testing of pairwise drug combinations, which, however, faces a combinatorial challenge: for 1000 U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA)-approved drugs, there are 499,500 possible pairwise combinations that should be tested over approximately 3000 human diseases and multiple dosage combinations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Crystal, A. S. et al. Patient-derived models of acquired resistance can identify effective drug combinations for cancer. Science 346, 1480–1486 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4768e581">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tan, X. et al. Systematic identification of synergistic drug pairs targeting HIV. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 1125–1130 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4768e584">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Lipinski, C. &amp; Hopkins, A. Navigating chemical space for biology and medicine. Nature 432, 855–861 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4768e587">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bulusu, K. C. et al. Modelling of compound combination effects and applications to efficacy and toxicity: state-of-the-art, challenges and perspectives. Drug Discov. Today 21, 225–238 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4768e590">9</a></sup>. We are, therefore, far from even a cursory exploration of the vast number of possible combinations with potentially positive clinical outcomes. To be sure, several machine learning-based “black-box” models have been developed to predict drug combinations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, Y. et al. Combining genomic and network characteristics for extended capability in predicting synergistic drugs for cancer. Nat. Commun. 6, 8481 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4768e594">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bansal, M. et al. A community computational challenge to predict the activity of pairs of compounds. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1213–1222 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4768e594_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zimmer, A., Katzir, I., Dekel, E., Mayo, A. E. &amp; Alon, U. Prediction of multidimensional drug dose responses based on measurements of drug pairs. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 10442–10447 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4768e597">12</a></sup>, offering a modest increase in accuracy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Sun, Y. et al. Combining genomic and network characteristics for extended capability in predicting synergistic drugs for cancer. Nat. Commun. 6, 8481 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4768e601">10</a></sup> over random guesses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bansal, M. et al. A community computational challenge to predict the activity of pairs of compounds. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1213–1222 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4768e605">11</a></sup>. We lack, however, predictive, mechanism-driven, network-medicine-based approaches to predict efficacious drug combinations.</p><p>Network-based approaches have already offered a promising framework to identify novel insights to accelerate drug discovery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Barabasi, A. L. &amp; Oltvai, Z. N. Network biology: understanding the cell’s functional organization. Nat. Rev. Genet. 5, 101–113 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4768e612">13</a></sup>, helping us quantify both disease–disease<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Menche, J. et al. Disease networks. Uncovering disease–disease relationships through the incomplete interactome. Science 347, 1257601 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4768e616">14</a></sup> and drug–disease<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guney, E., Menche, J., Vidal, M. &amp; Barabasi, A. L. Network-based in silico drug efficacy screening. Nat. Commun. 7, 10331 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4768e620">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Cheng, F. et al. Network-based approach to prediction and population-based validation of in silico drug repurposing. Nat. Commun. 9, 2691 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4768e623">16</a></sup> relationships. These methodological advances have raised the possibility of moving beyond the “one-drug, one-target” paradigm and exploring the “multiple-drugs, multiple-targets” possibilities offered by aiming at simultaneously modulating multiple disease proteins within the same disease module, while minimizing toxicity profiles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barabasi, A. L., Gulbahce, N. &amp; Loscalzo, J. Network medicine: a network-based approach to human disease. Nat. Rev. Genet. 12, 56–68 (2011)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4768e627">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vidal, M., Cusick, M. E. &amp; Barabasi, A. L. Interactome networks and human disease. Cell 144, 986–998 (2011)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4768e627_1">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yildirim, M. A., Goh, K. I., Cusick, M. E., Barabasi, A. L. &amp; Vidal, M. Drug–target network. Nat. Biotechnol. 25, 1119–1126 (2007)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4768e627_2">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hopkins, A. L. Network pharmacology: the next paradigm in drug discovery. Nat. Chem. Biol. 4, 682–690 (2008)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4768e627_3">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hopkins, A. L. Network pharmacology. Nat. Biotechnol. 25, 1110–1111 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4768e630">21</a></sup>. In this study, we quantify the relationship between drug targets and disease proteins in the human protein–protein interactome, leading to a rational, network-based drug combination design strategy.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec3">Network-based proximity measure of drug–drug relationships</h3><p>Disease proteins are not scattered randomly in the interactome, but tend to form localized neighborhoods, known as disease modules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Menche, J. et al. Disease networks. Uncovering disease–disease relationships through the incomplete interactome. Science 347, 1257601 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4768e646">14</a></sup>. An efficient way to capture network proximity between a drug (<i>X</i>) and a disease (<i>Y</i>) is by the <i>z</i>-score (<span class="mathjax-tex">\(z = \frac{{{d} - {\mu }}}{{\sigma }}\)</span>), which relies on the shortest path lengths <i>d</i>(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>) between drug targets (<i>x</i>) and disease proteins (<i>y</i>).</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{d}}\left( {X,Y} \right) = \frac{1}{{\left\Vert Y \right\Vert}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{{\mathrm{y}} \in Y} {\mathrm{min}}_{{\mathrm{x}} \in X}{\mathrm{d}}({\mathrm{x}},{\mathrm{y}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><p>The <i>z</i>-score is obtained by comparing the observed distance to a reference distance distribution between a randomly selected group of proteins of matching size and degree distribution as the disease proteins and drug targets in the human interactome. The <i>z</i>-score is applicable when the reference distribution is well described by a Gaussian (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>) and was successfully used to identify well-known and new drug–disease relationships in the human interactome for monotherapy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guney, E., Menche, J., Vidal, M. &amp; Barabasi, A. L. Network-based in silico drug efficacy screening. Nat. Commun. 7, 10331 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4768e838">15</a></sup>. Here, we hypothesize that exploring the network-based relationship between two drugs and their targets, and the disease proteins in the disease module would help clarify the mechanism-of-action of effective drug combinations while minimizing adverse effects (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>To test our hypothesis, we assembled 243,603 experimentally confirmed protein–protein interactions (PPIs) connecting 16,677 unique proteins from five data sources (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM3">1</a>). We also compiled 1978 FDA-approved or clinically investigational drugs that have at least two experimentally reported targets by pooling the high-quality drug–target binding affinity profiles from six data sources (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM4">2</a>). In order to fully characterize the mutual relationship of two drugs and a disease module, we need a network-based proximity measure between the two drugs’ targets, as well. Although it has not been tested for this purpose, in principle, we could rely on the <i>z</i>-score for this task. However, in contrast to the relatively large disease modules, each drug has only a small number of experimentally reported targets (on average 3, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">2</a>). Therefore, the randomization procedure is not producing a Gaussian distribution as described in our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guney, E., Menche, J., Vidal, M. &amp; Barabasi, A. L. Network-based in silico drug efficacy screening. Nat. Commun. 7, 10331 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4768e864">15</a></sup>, limiting the applicability of the <i>z</i>-score. Indeed, we find that the <i>z</i>-score cannot discriminate FDA-approved pairwise combinations or clinically reported adverse drug interactions from random drug pairs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">3</a>). Instead of relying on randomization, therefore, we measure the network proximity of drug–target modules A, B as reflected in their target localizations using the recently introduced separation measure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Menche, J. et al. Disease networks. Uncovering disease–disease relationships through the incomplete interactome. Science 347, 1257601 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4768e877">14</a></sup>:</p><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{s}}_{{\mathrm{AB}}} \equiv \left\langle {{\mathrm{d}}_{{\mathrm{AB}}}} \right\rangle - \frac{{\left\langle {{\mathrm{d}}_{{\mathrm{AA}}}} \right\rangle + \left\langle {{\mathrm{d}}_{{\mathrm{BB}}}} \right\rangle }}{2}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (2)
                </div></div><p>which compares the mean shortest distance within the interactome between the targets of each drug, 〈d<sub>AA</sub>〉 and 〈d<sub>BB</sub>〉, to the mean shortest distance 〈d<sub>AB</sub>〉 between A–B target pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1a</a>). In 〈d<sub>AB</sub>〉, targets associated with both drugs A and B have a zero distance by definition. For s<sub>AB</sub> &lt; 0, the targets of the two drugs are located in the same network neighborhood (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1b</a>), while for s<sub>AB</sub> ≥ 0, the two drug targets are topologically separated (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1c</a>). For example, imatinib (I) is an FDA-approved agent for the treatment of chronic myeloid leukemia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kalmanti, L. et al. Safety and efficacy of imatinib in CML over a period of 10 years: data from the randomized CML-study IV. Leukemia 29, 1123–1132 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1018">22</a></sup>. Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1a, b</a> shows that imatinib’s targets are in the same network neighborhood as the targets of tandutinib (T), a FMS-like tyrosine kinase 3 (FLT3) inhibitor under phase III trial for treating acute myeloid leukemia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="DeAngelo, D. J. et al. Phase 1 clinical results with tandutinib (MLN518), a novel FLT3 antagonist, in patients with acute myelogenous leukemia or high-risk myelodysplastic syndrome: safety, pharmacokinetics, and pharmacodynamics. Blood 108, 3674–3681 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1025">23</a></sup>; consequently the separation score between their targets is negative, s<sub>IT</sub> = −0.35. However, the targets of natalizumab (N), an FDA-approved monoclonal antibody for treating multiple sclerosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Polman, C. H. et al. A randomized, placebo-controlled trial of natalizumab for relapsing multiple sclerosis. N. Engl. J. Med. 354, 899–910 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1032">24</a></sup>, are in a topologically distinct neighborhood from the targets of imatinib and tandutinib, having a positive s<sub>IN</sub> = 0.59 and s<sub>TN</sub> = 0.49, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Network-based model of drug–drug relationship. <b>a</b> A subnetwork of the human interactome illustrating the network-based relationship between drug targets associated with three drugs (imatinib [I], tandutinib [T], and natalizumab [N]). <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> The definition of drug pairs that are topologically overlapping (s<sub>AB</sub> &lt; 0, <b>b</b>) or topologically separated (s<sub>AB</sub> ≥ 0, <b>c</b>). <b>d</b>–<b>j</b> The interplay between topological separation of drug pairs and five types of drug profiles: drug–drug chemical similarity (<b>d</b>); drug target-encoding gene co-expression pattern across human tissues (<b>e</b>); drug target protein sequence similarity (<b>f</b>). Using the Gene Ontology (GO) annotations, we determine for each drug how similar its associated target-encoding genes are in terms of their biological processes (<b>g</b>), cellular component (<b>h</b>), and molecular function (<b>i</b>); and clinical similarity (<b>j</b>) of drug pairs derived from Anatomical Therapeutic Chemical Classification Systems codes (see Methods). Overlapping drug pairs are highlighted in orange (s<sub>AB</sub> &lt; 0); topologically separated drug pairs are highlighted in blue (s<sub>AB</sub> ≥ 0)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>For a network-based approach to drug combinations to be effective, we need to establish if the topological relationship between two drug–target modules, as captured by s<sub>AB</sub>, reflects biological and pharmacological relationships, as well. We find that the network proximity (s<sub>AB</sub>; Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ2">2</a>)) of the targets of drug–drug pairs in the human interactome correlates with chemical, biological, functional, and clinical similarities (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1d–j</a>), outperforming the target-overlap approaches (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">4</a>). This suggests that each drug–target module has a well-defined network-based footprint. If the footprints of two drug–target modules are topologically separated (s<sub>AB</sub> ≥ 0), then the drugs are pharmacologically distinct. If the footprints of two drug–target modules overlap (s<sub>AB</sub> &lt; 0), the magnitude of the overlap is indicative of their pharmacological relationship: closer network proximity of targets of a drug pair indicates higher similarities in their chemical, biological, functional, and clinical profiles (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">5</a>). In contrast, we find that the <i>z</i>-score measure of the targets of drug–drug pairs in the human interactome does not correlate with chemical, biological, functional, and clinical similarities (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">6</a>). We also compared network proximity (s<sub>AB</sub>) against five other network-based measures between targets of drug–drug pairs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">7</a>): (1) separation (Eq.  (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ2">2</a>)), (2) closest, (3) shortest, (4) kernel, and (5) centre (see Methods). Relying on 681 FDA-approved or experimentally validated pairwise drug combinations across all human diseases (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>), we find that FDA-approved drug combinations have lower s<sub>AB</sub> compared to random drug pairs and the separation-based measure outperforms all four alternative measures as well as traditional chemoinformatics and bioinformatics approaches (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">9</a>), confirming that s<sub>AB</sub> offers a reliable measure of drug–drug relationships within the human interactome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec4">Network configurations of drug–drug–disease combinations</h3><p>Next, we turn to quantify the network-based relationship between two drug–target modules and a disease module (drug–drug–disease combinations). We find that from a network perspective, all possible drug–drug–disease combinations can be classified into six topologically distinct classes: (a) Overlapping Exposure: Two overlapping drug–target modules that also overlap with the disease module of interest (P1 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2a)</a>; (b) Complementary Exposure: Two separated drug–target modules that overlap individually with the disease module (P2 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a>); (c) Indirect Exposure: One drug–target module of two overlapping drug–target modules overlaps with the disease module (P3 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2c</a>); (d) Single Exposure: One drug–target module separated from another drug–target module overlaps with the disease module (P4 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2d</a>); (e) Non-exposure: Two overlapping drug–target modules are topologically separated from the disease module (P5 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2e</a>); and (f) Independent Action: Each of the drug–target modules and the disease module are topologically separated (P6 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2f</a>). The question is, do these six classes manifest in detectable differences in clinical efficacy for drug combinations?</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The efficacy of hypertensive drug–drug interactions. <b>a</b>–<b>f</b> Schematic diagrams of the six distinct classes capturing the network-based relationship between two drug–target modules and one disease module on a drug–drug–disease combination. For s<sub>AB</sub> &lt; 0, the two sets of drug targets overlap topologically (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1b</a>); while for s<sub>AB</sub> ≥ 0, the two sets of drug targets are separated topologically (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig1">1c</a>). The <i>z</i>-scores (<i>z</i>), measuring the drug–disease separation, are calculated for quantifying the significance of the shortest paths between drug targets and disease proteins in the human protein–protein interactome. For <i>z</i> &lt; 0, the drug–target module and the disease module overlap; while for <i>z</i> ≥ 0, the drug–target module and the disease module are separated. Color histograms (Real) show the antihypertensive combinations (purple) and clinically reported adverse drug interactions on high blood pressure (blue), respectively. We assembled the antihypertensive combinations from three types of experimental evidences: (i) FDA-approved evidence, (ii) clinical data from Clinicaltrials.gov database, and (iii) preclinical studies from literature (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>). We randomly selected the same number of adverse drug–drug interactions related to high blood pressure from 1512 clinically reported adverse drug–drug interactions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM6">4</a>) corresponding to the number of antihypertensive combinations using a bootstrapping algorithm in R software and this process was repeated 100 times (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">5</a>). Gray boxes (Random) show random expectation. Error bars indicate the standard deviation. The <i>P</i>-value (<i>P</i>) is calculated by testing 10,000 permutations (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">5</a>). The network-based relationships between two drug–target modules and one disease module for FDA-approved hypertensive drug combinations only are illustrated in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>To understand which of these drug–drug–disease configurations have the greatest clinical efficacy, we focus on hypertension and cancer, two diseases with a large number of FDA-approved pairwise combinations (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>). We find that four out of six drug–drug–disease configurations (P3–P6 in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2c–f</a>) do not show a statistically significant tendency to co-treat cancer (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">10c</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">10f</a>) or hypertension (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2c–f</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11c</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11f</a>). In other words, if at least one drug in a combination fails to be localized to the vicinity of the disease module, the combination does not have a therapeutic effect greater than monotherapy. This leads to our first major finding: for a drug pair to have a therapeutic effect, both drug–target modules must overlap with the disease module. This finding highlights the need to inspect the network-relationship between drug targets and disease proteins as we search for therapeutically beneficial combinations.</p><p>The second finding is that Overlapping Exposure, i.e., when the drug–target modules overlap with each other as well as with the disease module, has no statistically significant efficacy in treating the disease over monotherapy. Overlapping Exposure does, however, have statistically significant adverse effects, such as causing high blood pressure (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM6">4</a>) compared to the control group (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test, Overlapping Exposure [P1], Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2a</a>). For example, two FDA-approved hypertensive drugs, nadroparin and spironolactone, fall into the Overlapping Exposure (P1, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Tab1">1</a>) category with the hypertension disease module, in line with the observation that nadroparin increased the hyperkalemic effect (adverse effect) of spironolactone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wolf, G. &amp; Stahl, R. A. A woman with increasing weakness of the legs and irregular heartbeat who took her husband’s medication. Nephrol. Dial. Transplant. 17, 1698–1701 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1305">25</a></sup>. Cancer is a chronic disease with a strong genetic contribution, rarely caused by adverse drug–drug interactions. We therefore limit our testing of Overlapping Exposure in three cardiovascular outcomes, finding that Overlapping Exposure has statistically significant adverse effects on three cardiovascular outcomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">12</a>): arrhythmia (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test), heart failure (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test), and myocardial infarction (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test), consistent with previously reported overlapping toxicities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, Y. et al. Overcoming EGFR(T790M) and EGFR(C797S) resistance with mutant-selective allosteric inhibitors. Nature 534, 129–132 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1328">26</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Network configurations of the selected hypertensive drug–drug pairs</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span></a></div></figure></div><p>The third key finding is that only drug pairs that have a Complementary Exposure relationship to the disease module (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">10b</a>) show a statistically significant efficacy for drug combination therapies. Consider, for example, amiloride and hydrochlorothiazide, in the Complementary Exposure (P2 in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Tab1">1</a>) class. This combination has been shown to prevent glucose intolerance and to improve blood pressure control compared with monotherapy with either drug in the PATHWAY-3 trial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Brown, M. J. et al. Effect of amiloride, or amiloride plus hydrochlorothiazide, versus hydrochlorothiazide on glucose tolerance and blood pressure (PATHWAY-3): a parallel-group, double-blind randomised phase 4 trial. Lancet Diabetes Endocrinol. 4, 136–147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1692">27</a></sup>. In other words, we find that only separated drugs that individually overlap with the disease module show statistical significance in co-treatment of hypertension (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test, Complementary Exposure in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11b</a>) and similarly for cancer (<i>P</i> &lt; 1.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, permutation test, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">10b</a>). Specifically, we assembled hypertensive drug combination data from FDA-approved evidence, clinical trials from the Clinicaltrials.gov database, and pre-clinical studies from literature curation (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>), finding again that drug pairs with Complementary Exposure (P2) significantly tend to co-treat hypertension efficiently (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a>) compared to FDA-approved antihypertensive combinations (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11b</a>), indicating low selection bias. The unique network-based relationship that drug pairs with Complementary Exposure to the disease module tend to be effective drug combinations is consistent with the non-overlapping pharmacological principle in rational drug combination design<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, Y. et al. Overcoming EGFR(T790M) and EGFR(C797S) resistance with mutant-selective allosteric inhibitors. Nature 534, 129–132 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1726">26</a></sup>. In summary, characterizing the network-based relationship between drug–target modules and the disease module (Complementary Exposure) within the human interactome offers a powerful, network-based strategy for rational drug combination design.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec5">Network-based uncovering of hypertensive drug combinations</h3><p>The finding that Complementary Exposure is predictive of effective drug combinations prompts us to offer network-based predictions of new drug combinations using hypertension data as a validation set (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>). Such a predictor builds on two established network approaches: (a) network-based separation (Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ2">2</a>)) between targets of two drugs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Menche, J. et al. Disease networks. Uncovering disease–disease relationships through the incomplete interactome. Science 347, 1257601 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1744">14</a></sup>; and (b) network proximity (Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ1">1</a>)) between the disease (hypertension) module and the two drug–target modules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guney, E., Menche, J., Vidal, M. &amp; Barabasi, A. L. Network-based in silico drug efficacy screening. Nat. Commun. 7, 10331 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1751">15</a></sup>. Specifically, we rank all possible drug pairs by increasing separation score (s<sub>AB</sub>). We then restrict this list to drug pairs with Complementary Exposure (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a>) to the hypertension disease module. In principle, this approach allows us to identify pairwise combinations not only between two hypertensive drugs, but also between one hypertensive drug and one non-hypertensive drug, or between two non-hypertensive drugs. Here, we focus on drug combinations involving 65 FDA-approved hypertensive drugs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig3">3a</a>). The 24 FDA-approved combinations involving hypertensive drugs (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>) lead to a 59% accuracy (AUC = 0.589 ± 0.002) for the network-based discrimination of the approved hypertensive drug combinations from random drug pairs. This network-based approach outperforms traditional chemoinformatics (AUC = 0.489 ± 0.002) and bioinformatics approaches (AUC = 0.529 ± 0.002, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">13</a>), indicating that network proximity offers an efficacious strategy to identify new drug combinations for treatment of hypertension.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09186-x/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9186_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Network-based stratification of hypertensive drug combinations. <b>a</b> Heatmap showing the predicted network-based separation (s<sub>AB</sub>) among 65 FDA-approved anti-hypertensive drugs. Drugs are clustered based on their target families. FDA-approved or experimentally validated drug combinations for the treatment of hypertension are highlighted by circles (o). Clinically reported adverse drug interactions are highlighted by X. Color keys are shown by s<sub>AB</sub>. The right heatmap highlighting the four distinct classes capturing the network-based relationship (P1–P4, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2a–d</a>) for 65 FDA-approved anti-hypertensive drugs on the hypertension disease module. <b>b</b> A network map showing the relationship between the drug–target modules and the hypertension disease module (the largest connected subgraph by red) in the human interactome. Complementary Exposure for two FDA-approved drug combinations (hydrochlorothiazide–amiloride and hydrochlorothiazide–spironolactone) are highlighted by blue and orange via blue arrow, respectively. Overlapping Exposure for two clinically reported adverse drug–drug interactions (diazoxide–hydrochlorothiazide and diazoxide–bendroflumethiazide) are also illustrated by red arrow. The target families of 65 FDA-approved anti-hypertensive drugs are highlighted by different colors in both (<b>a</b>) and (<b>b</b>). The edges in (<b>b</b>) denote the protein–protein interactions (PPIs) colored by different types of hypertensive drug target families or known hypertension disease proteins (genes)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-category="article body" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>To exploit the predictive power of this network-based prediction prospectively, we first focus on hydrochlorothiazide, an FDA-approved inhibitor on the sodium-chloride symporter for treatment of hypertension<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Beermann, B., Groschinsky-Grind, M. &amp; Rosen, A. Absorption, metabolism, and excretion of hydrochlorothiazide. Clin. Pharmacol. Ther. 19, 531–537 (1976)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1814">28</a></sup>. Our network-based algorithm offers 30 potentially efficacious combinations involving hydrochlorothiazide, of which 21 (70% success rate) are already validated by FDA-approved evidence, clinical studies from Clinicaltrials.gov, or previously reported preclinical data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>). Specifically, we restrict the predicted drug pairs on hydrochlorothiazide with Complementary Exposure and select the top 30 candidates ranked based on their increasing separation score (s<sub>AB</sub>). For example, the predicted spironolactone and hydrochlorothiazide combination has been approved for treating hypertension and fluid retention in patients with congestive heart failure or nephrotic syndrome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Tab1">1</a>). Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig3">3b</a> shows the network-based relationship between targets of spironolactone and hydrochlorothiazide within the hypertension disease module. We find that hydrochlorothiazide targets the sodium-chloride symporter pathway (e.g., SLC12A3) and spironolactone targets the mineralocorticoid receptor pathway (e.g., NR3C2), both in the hypertension disease module following by the Complementary Exposure category (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig3">3b</a>). Avapro, a combination of irbesartan (an FDA-approved angiotensin receptor blocker for the treatment of hypertension) and hydrochlorothiazide, has been approved for the treatment of hypertension (Application No. 20758S003, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>) is predicted to be an efficacious combination by this approach. Nifedipine, an approved voltage-dependent calcium channel blocker for the treatment of hypertension, is another network-predicted combination partner for hydrochlorothiazide. A recent preclinical study reported that combining nifedipine (10 mg/kg/day) with hydrochlorothiazide (10 mg/kg/day) showed a significant synergistic effect on blood pressure reduction, blood pressure variability enhancement, and organ protection in spontaneously hypertensive rats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Han, P., Chu, Z. X., Shen, F. M., Xie, H. H. &amp; Su, D. F. Synergism of hydrochlorothiazide and nitrendipine on reduction of blood pressure and blood pressure variability in spontaneously hypertensive rats. Acta Pharmacol. Sin. 27, 1575–1579 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1839">29</a></sup>. Telmisartan, an angiotensin II receptor antagonist, is the other network-predicted combination partner for hydrochlorothiazide. Multiple clinical trials registered in Clinicaltrials.gov are under way or have been completed that reveal potentially therapeutic combination effects of telmisartan and hydrochlorothiazide on the treatment of hypertension (NCT00509470, NCT00239369, and NCT00144222). Altogether, the network-based model has successfully identified well-known hypertensive drug combinations with well-defined drug pharmacological pathways in the hypertension disease module (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><p>Finally, our approach helps us computationally identify several drug combinations for the treatment of hypertension. For example, ethacrynic acid, a sodium/potassium/chloride symporter inhibitor, is approved by FDA to treat hypertension, congestive heart failure, and kidney failure. Our network-based model reveals that ethacrynic acid has the highest likelihood for validating combination with hydrochlorothiazide to treat hypertension (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>), representing a potential drug combination. Guanfacine, an approved alpha-2 adrenoreceptor inhibitor to treat hypertension and attention deficit hyperactivity disorder, is predicted to have a high likelihood when used together with hydrochlorothiazide (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">1</a>), representing another potential drug combination for the treatment of hypertension. In total, we computationally identified 1455 potential combinations involving 65 hypertensive drugs with Complementary Exposure to the hypertension disease module (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM7">5</a>). In addition, we provide an exhaustive list of predicted drug combinations involving non-hypertensive drugs with Complementary Exposure and potential adverse drug interactions involving drug pairs with Overlapping Exposure to the hypertension disease module (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM7">5</a>), offering a potential virtual hypertensive drug combination database for future experimental validation and prospective clinical trials. Taken together, the network-based models developed here offer a powerful tool to identify efficacious drug combinations for the treatment of hypertension.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="Sec6-content"><p>Combination therapies offer widespread well-documented advantages in the treatment of complex diseases. Here, we demonstrated that a network-based methodology that identifies the relative network configuration of drug–target modules with respect to the disease module can help prioritize potentially efficacious pairwise drug combinations for both hypertension and cancer. Specifically, our interactome-based approach offers a network-level view of therapeutic combinations in terms of comparative efficacy and adverse interactions. Our key finding is that a drug combination is therapeutically effective only if it follows a specific network topological relationship to the disease module, as captured by the Complementary Exposure pattern. Somewhat surprisingly, if we are searching for therapeutically synergistic combinations, the two drug–target modules must not only overlap with the disease module, but also need to be separated in the human interactome without overlapping toxicities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Jia, Y. et al. Overcoming EGFR(T790M) and EGFR(C797S) resistance with mutant-selective allosteric inhibitors. Nature 534, 129–132 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1870">26</a></sup>. Here we documented the predictive power of Complementary Exposure in two complex diseases (hypertension and cancer) based on known drug combination data from publicly available databases. Therefore, future work is needed to explore the generalizability of our findings to other diseases.</p><p>Drug pairs with Complementary Exposure are expected to have either therapeutic (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig2">2b</a>) or adverse effects (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">12b</a>). For example, a recent clinical study has reported that combining amiloride and hydrochlorothiazide (Complementary Exposure in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Fig3">3b</a>) induced hypokalemia (adverse effect) in hypertensive patients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Maronde, R. F., Milgrom, M., Vlachakis, N. D. &amp; Chan, L. Response of thiazide-induced hypokalemia to amiloride. JAMA 249, 237–241 (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1889">30</a></sup>. Altogether, adverse effects can appear independently from the separation of the two drug target modules, occurring significantly in both Overlapping Exposure (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">12a</a>) and Complementary Exposure (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">11b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">12b</a>). Lack of dose-dependent information and precise perturbation effects of disease-causing variants and drug exposure generate a coupled interplay between adverse and therapeutic effects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Greene, J. A. &amp; Loscalzo, J. Putting the patient back together—social medicine, network medicine, and the limits of reductionism. N. Engl. J. Med. 377, 2493–2499 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1906">31</a></sup> for Complementary Exposure. Integration of network proximity, high-throughput in vitro or in vivo assays, pharmacokinetics-based mathematical modeling (e.g., cytochrome P450 enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Bibi, Z. Role of cytochrome P450 in drug interactions. Nutr. Metab. 5, 27 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1910">32</a></sup>), large-scale pharmacointeraction networks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Cami, A., Manzi, S., Arnold, A. &amp; Reis, B. Y. Pharmacointeraction network models predict unknown drug–drug interactions. PLoS ONE 8, e61468 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1915">33</a></sup>, and patient data (i.e., health insurance claims and electronic health records)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Cheng, F. et al. Network-based approach to prediction and population-based validation of in silico drug repurposing. Nat. Commun. 9, 2691 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1919">16</a></sup> could establish the causal mechanism and clinical evidence through which drug combinations could be identified with specific therapeutic indications without obvious adverse effects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhao, S. et al. Systems pharmacology of adverse event mitigation by drug combinations. Sci. Transl. Med. 5, 206ra140 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1923">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tatonetti, N. P., Ye, P. P., Daneshjou, R. &amp; Altman, R. B. Data-driven prediction of drug effects and interactions. Sci. Transl. Med. 4, 125ra131 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1926">35</a></sup>. In addition, future work is needed to explore the effects of potential data selection bias. For example, combinations of drugs that target related proteins in the same disease module are more likely to be tested in drug combination clinical trials. Finally, given the lack of large-scale, systematic data on combinations of multiple drugs, in our current study, we limited our exploration on drug pairs only. Yet, we expect that Complementary Exposure remains an efficient design principle even upon combining multiple drugs. To fight the combinatorial explosion upon inspecting network relationships of multiple drug–target modules with a disease module, theories of signed networks can be of great help, such as structural balance theories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Facchetti, G., Iacono, G. &amp; Altafini, C. Computing global structural balance in large-scale signed social networks. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A. 108, 20953–20958 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1930">36</a></sup>, reducing the number of network patterns to investigate in the human interactome. In addition, advanced network-based link prediction methods rooted in biological principles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Istvan, A. K. et al. Network-based prediction of protein interactions. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/275529&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1934">37</a></sup> can help to develop a combined, quantitative score for each predicted drug combination. Eventually, experimental validation and prospective clinical trials must be conducted to verify the network-predicted drug combinations under controlled conditions. As for the input data, having a more complete human interactome and more complete, systematic drug–target network with well-annotated pharmacokinetics and pharmacodynamics information would improve the performance of the network-based model further.</p><p>In summary, our findings suggest that the discovery of efficacious drug combinations could benefit from network-based, rational drug combination screenings, exploring the relationship between drug–target modules and the disease modules via network proximity in the human interactome. From a translational perspective, the network tools developed here could help develop novel, efficacious combination therapies for multiple complex diseases if broadly applied.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec8">Constructing the human protein–protein interactome</h3><p>We assembled 15 commonly used databases, focusing on high-quality PPIs with five types of evidences: (1) binary, physical PPIs tested by high-throughput yeast-two-hybrid (Y2H) screening system, combining binary PPIs tested from two publicly available high-quality Y2H datasets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Rolland, T. et al. A proteome-scale map of the human interactome network. Cell 159, 1212–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1953">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Rual, J. F. et al. Towards a proteome-scale map of the human protein-protein interaction network. Nature 437, 1173–1178 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1956">39</a></sup>, and one unpublished dataset, available at <a href="http://ccsb.dana-farber.org/interactome-data.html">http://ccsb.dana-farber.org/interactome-data.html</a>; (2) literature-curated PPIs identified by affinity purification followed by affinity-purification mass spectrometry (AP-MS), Y2H, and literature-derived low-throughput experiments; (3) binary, physical PPIs derived from protein three-dimensional structures; (4) kinase-substrate interactions by literature-derived low-throughput and high-throughput experiments; and (5) signaling networks by literature-derived low-throughput experiments. The protein-coding genes were mapped to their official gene symbols based on GeneCards (<a href="http://www.genecards.org/">http://www.genecards.org/</a>) and their Entrez ID. Computationally inferred interactions rooted in evolutionary analysis, gene expression data, and metabolic associations were excluded. The updated human interactome includes 243,603 PPIs connecting 16,677 unique proteins, and is 40% greater in size compared to our previously used human interactome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Menche, J. et al. Disease networks. Uncovering disease–disease relationships through the incomplete interactome. Science 347, 1257601 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4768e1974">14</a></sup>. The human protein–protein interactome are provided in the Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM3">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec9">Construction of drug–target network</h3><p>We collected high-quality physical drug–target interactions on FDA-approved or clinically investigational drugs from 6 commonly used data sources, and defined a physical drug–target interaction using reported binding affinity data: inhibition constant/potency (<i>K</i><sub>i</sub>), dissociation constant (<i>K</i><sub>d</sub>), median effective concentration (EC<sub>50</sub>), or median inhibitory concentration (IC<sub>50</sub>) ≤ 10 µM. Drug–target interactions were acquired from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al. DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2002">40</a></sup>, the Therapeutic Target Database (TTD, v4.3.02)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhu, F. et al. Therapeutic target database update 2012: a resource for facilitating target-oriented drug discovery. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1128–D1136 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2006">41</a></sup>, and the PharmGKB database (December 30, 2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hernandez-Boussard, T. et al. The pharmacogenetics and pharmacogenomics knowledge base: accentuating the knowledge. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D913–D918 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2010">42</a></sup>. Specifically, bioactivity data of drug–target pairs were collected from three widely used databases: ChEMBL (v20, accessed in December 2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gaulton, A. et al. ChEMBL: a large-scale bioactivity database for drug discovery. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1100–D1107 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2014">43</a></sup>, BindingDB (downloaded in December 2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Liu, T. Q., Lin, Y. M., Wen, X., Jorissen, R. N. &amp; Gilson, M. K. BindingDB: a web-accessible database of experimentally determined protein–ligand binding affinities. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D198–D201 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2018">44</a></sup>, and IUPHAR/BPS Guide to PHARMACOLOGY (downloaded in December 2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pawson, A. J. et al. The IUPHAR/BPS Guide to PHARMACOLOGY: an expert-driven knowledgebase of drug targets and their ligands. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1098–D1106 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2022">45</a></sup>. After extracting the bioactivity data related to drugs from these databases, we retained only the drug–target interactions that meet the following four criteria: (i) binding affinities, including <i>K</i><sub>i</sub>, <i>K</i><sub>d</sub>, IC<sub>50</sub> or EC<sub>50</sub> each ≤10 μM; (ii) proteins can be represented by unique UniProt accession number; (iii) proteins are marked as “reviewed” in the UniProt database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Apweiler, R. et al. UniProt: the universal protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D115–D119 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2039">46</a></sup>; and (iv) proteins are from Homo sapiens. In total, 15,051 drug–target interactions connecting 4428 drugs and 2256 unique human targets were built, including 1978 drugs that have at least two experimentally validated targets (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec10">Collecting gold-standard pairwise drug combinations</h3><p>In this study, we focused on pairwise drug combinations by assembling the clinical data from the multiple data sources (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">3</a>). Each drug in combinations was required to have the experimentally validated target information: each EC<sub>50</sub>, IC<sub>50</sub>, <i>K</i><sub>i</sub>, or <i>K</i><sub>d</sub> ≤ 10 µM. Compound name, generic name, or commercial name of each drug was standardized by MeSH and UMLS vocabularies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bodenreider, O. The Unified Medical Language System (UMLS): integrating biomedical terminology. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D267–D270 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2071">47</a></sup> and further transferred to DrugBank ID from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al.
