DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2075">40</a></sup>. Duplicated drug pairs were removed. In total, 681 unique pairwise drug combinations connecting 362 drugs were retained (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec11">Collecting adverse drug–drug interactions</h3><p>We compiled clinically reported adverse drug–drug interactions (DDIs) data from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al. DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2090">40</a></sup>. Here, we focused on adverse drug interactions where each drug has the experimentally validated target information. Compound name, generic name, or commercial name of each drug were standardized by MeSH and UMLS vocabularies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bodenreider, O. The Unified Medical Language System (UMLS): integrating biomedical terminology. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D267–D270 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2094">47</a></sup> and further transferred to DrugBank ID from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al. DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2098">40</a></sup>. In total, 13,397 clinically reported adverse DDIs connecting 658 unique drugs were retained (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM6">4</a>). In addition, we collected cardiovascular event-specific adverse DDIs from the TWOSIDE database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tatonetti, N. P., Ye, P. P., Daneshjou, R. &amp; Altman, R. B. Data-driven prediction of drug effects and interactions. Sci. Transl. Med. 4, 125ra131 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2105">35</a></sup>. TWOSIDE includes over 868,221 significant associations connecting 59,220 drug pairs and 1301 adverse events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tatonetti, N. P., Ye, P. P., Daneshjou, R. &amp; Altman, R. B. Data-driven prediction of drug effects and interactions. Sci. Transl. Med. 4, 125ra131 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2110">35</a></sup>. In this study, we focused on 4 types of cardiovascular events: arrhythmia (MeSH ID: D001145), heart failure (MeSH ID: D006333), myocardial infarction (MeSH ID: D009203), and high blood pressure (MeSH ID: D006973).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec12">Chemical similarity analysis of drug pairs</h3><p>We downloaded chemical structure information (SMILES format) from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al. DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2123">40</a></sup> and computed MACCS fingerprints of each drug using Open Babel v2.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="O’Boyle, N. M. et al. Open Babel: an open chemical toolbox. J. Cheminform. 3, 33 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2127">48</a></sup>. If two drug molecules have a and b bits set in their MACCS fragment bit-strings, with c of these bits being set in the fingerprints of both drugs, the Tanimoto coefficient (T) of a drug–drug pair is defined as:</p><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{T}} = \frac{c}{{a + b - c}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (3)
                </div></div><p>T is widely used in drug discovery and development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Willett, P. Similarity-based virtual screening using 2D fingerprints. Drug Discov. Today 11, 1046–1053 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2176">49</a></sup>, offering a value in the range of zero (no bits in common) to one (all bits are the same).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec13">Protein sequence similarity (identity) analysis</h3><p>We downloaded the canonical protein sequences of drug targets (proteins) in <i>Homo sapiens</i> from UniProt database (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>). We calculated the protein sequence similarity <i>S</i><sub><i>P</i></sub>(<i>a, b</i>) of two drug targets a and b using the Smith–Waterman algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Smith, T. F. &amp; Waterman, M. S. Identification of common molecular subsequences. J. Mol. Biol. 147, 195–197 (1981)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2207">50</a></sup>. The Smith–Waterman algorithm performs local sequence alignment by comparing segments of all possible lengths and optimizing the similarity measure for determining similar regions between two strings of protein canonical sequences of drug targets. The overall sequence similarity of the targets binding two drugs A and B is determined by Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ4">4</a>) by averaging all pairs of proteins a and b with <span class="mathjax-tex">\(a \in A\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(b \in B\)</span> under the condition <span class="mathjax-tex">\(a \ne b\)</span>. This condition ensures that for drugs with common targets we do not take pairs into account where a target would be compared to itself.</p><div id="Equ4" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\langle S_p \rangle = \frac{1}{{n_{\mathrm{pairs}}}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{\{ a,b\} } S_p\left( {a,b} \right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (4)
                </div></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec14">Gene co-expression analysis</h3><p>We downloaded the RNA-seq data (RPKM value) across 32 tissues from GTEx V6 release (accessed on April 2016, <a href="https://gtexportal.org/">https://gtexportal.org/</a>). For each tissue, we regarded those genes with RPKM ≥ 1 in more than 80% samples as tissue-expressed genes. To measure the extent to which drug target-coding genes (a and b) associated with the drug-treated diseases are co-expressed, we calculated the Pearson’s correlation coefficient (<span class="mathjax-tex">\(PCC\left( {a,b} \right)\)</span>) and the corresponding <i>P</i>-value via <i>F</i>-statistics for each pair of drug target-coding genes a and b across 32 human tissues. In order to reduce the noise of co-expression analysis, we mapped <i>PCC</i>(<i>a, b</i>) into the human protein–protein interactome network (Supplementary <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">Methods 2</a>) to build a co-expressed protein–protein interactome network as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Cheng, F. et al. Studying tumorigenesis through network evolution and somatic mutational perturbations in the cancer interactome. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 2156–2169 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2465">51</a></sup>. The co-expression similarity of the drug target-coding genes associated with two drugs A and B is computed by averaging <i>PCC</i>(<i>a,b</i>) over all pairs of targets a and b with <span class="mathjax-tex">\(a \in A\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(b \in B\)</span> as below:</p><div id="Equ5" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\langle S_{\mathrm{co}} \rangle = \frac{1}{{n_{\mathrm{pairs}}}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{\{ a,b\} } |PCC\left( {a,b} \right)|$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (5)
                </div></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec15">Gene Ontology (GO) similarity analysis</h3><p>The Gene Ontology (GO) annotation for all drug target-coding genes was downloaded from the website: <a href="http://www.geneontology.org/">http://www.geneontology.org/</a>. We used three types of the experimentally validated or literature-derived evidences: biological processes (BP), molecular function (MF), and cellular component (CC), excluding annotations inferred computationally. The semantic comparison of GO annotations offers quantitative ways to compute similarities between genes and gene products. We computed GO similarity <i>S</i><sub>GO</sub>(<i>a,b</i>) for each pair of drug target-coding genes a and b using a graph-based semantic similarity measure algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wang, J. Z., Du, Z., Payattakool, R., Yu, P. S. &amp; Chen, C. F. A new method to measure the semantic similarity of GO terms. Bioinformatics 23, 1274–1281 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2658">52</a></sup> implemented in an R package, named GOSemSim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Yu, G. et al. GOSemSim: an R package for measuring semantic similarity among GO terms and gene products. Bioinformatics 26, 976–978 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2662">53</a></sup>. The overall GO similarity of the drug target-coding genes binding to two drugs A and B was determined by Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ6">6</a>), averaging all pairs of drug target-coding genes a and b with <span class="mathjax-tex">\(a \in A\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(b \in B\)</span>.</p><div id="Equ6" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\langle S_{\mathrm{GO}} \rangle = \frac{1}{{n_{\mathrm{pairs}}}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{\{ a,b\} } S_{\mathrm{GO}}\left( {a,b} \right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (6)
                </div></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec16">Clinical similarity analysis</h3><p>Clinical similarities of drug pairs derived from the drug Anatomical Therapeutic Chemical (ATC) classification systems codes have been commonly used to predict new drug targets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Cheng, F. et al. Prediction of polypharmacological profiles of drugs by the integration of chemical, side effect, and therapeutic space. J. Chem. Inf. Model. 53, 753–762 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2841">54</a></sup>. The ATC codes for all FDA-approved drugs used in this study were downloaded from the DrugBank database (v4.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Law, V. et al. DrugBank 4.0: shedding new light on drug metabolism. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D1091–D1097 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4768e2845">40</a></sup>. The kth level drug clinical similarity (<i>S</i><sub><i>k</i></sub>) of drugs A and B is defined via the ATC codes as below.</p><div id="Equ7" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$S_k\left( {A,B} \right) = \frac{{ATC_k(A) \cap ATC_k(B)}}{{ATC_k(A) \cup ATC_k(B)}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (7)
                </div></div><p>where <i>ATC</i><sub><i>k</i></sub> represents all ATC codes at the <i>k</i>th level. A score <i>S</i><sub><i>atc</i></sub>(<i>A, B</i>) is used to define the clinical similarity between drugs A and B:</p><div id="Equ8" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$S_{atc}\left( {A,B} \right) = \frac{{\mathop {\sum }\nolimits_{k = 1}^n S_k\left( {A,B} \right)}}{n}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (8)
                </div></div><p>where <i>n</i> represents the five levels of ATC codes (ranging from 1 to 5). Note that drugs can have multiple ATC codes. For example, nicotine (a potent parasympathomimetic stimulant) has four different ATC codes: N07BA01, A11HA01, C04AC01, C10AD02. For a drug with multiple ATC codes, the clinical similarity was computed for each ATC code, and then, the average clinical similarity was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Cheng, F. et al. Prediction of polypharmacological profiles of drugs by the integration of chemical, side effect, and therapeutic space. J. Chem. Inf. Model. 53, 753–762 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4768e3163">54</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec17">Comparison with target set-overlapping approach</h3><p>In this section, we compared the introduced network-based separation (Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ2">2</a>)) of drugs with overlap measures that are solely based on shared targets, without using the PPI network. Here, we examined two measures to quantify the overlap between target sets of drug A and drug B:</p><div id="Equ9" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Overlap}}\;{\mathrm{coefficient}}\;C = \left| {A \cap B} \right|/\mathrm{min}(\left| A \right|,\left| B \right|)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (9)
                </div></div><div id="Equ10" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Jaccard - index}}\;J = \left| {A \cap B} \right|/\left| {A \cup B} \right|$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (10)
                </div></div><p>Both values range from 0 to 1: <i>J</i>, <i>C</i> = 0 revealing no common targets shared by the drugs. An overlap coefficient <i>C</i> = 1 indicates that one set is a complete subset of the other, where Jaccard-index <i>J</i> = 1 is for two identical target sets (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">4a</a>). Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">4c</a> show the distribution of <i>C</i> and <i>J</i> for all 1,955,253 drug pairs. The target-set overlap is low for most drug pairs, and the majority (96.8% = 1,892,455/1,955,253) do not share any targets. To investigate the statistical significance of the observed overlaps, we used a hypergeometric model. The null hypothesis is that drug targets are randomly located from the space of all N protein-coding genes in the human interactome. The overlap expected for two target sets A and B is then given by</p><div id="Equ11" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$c_{rand} = \frac{{\left| A \right| \times \left| B \right|}}{N}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (11)
                </div></div><p>For every observed overlap <span class="mathjax-tex">\(c_{obs} = \left| {A \cap B} \right|\)</span>, we then determined the fold-change</p><div id="Equ12" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$fc = \frac{{c_{obs}}}{{c_{rand}}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (12)
                </div></div><p>and the <i>P</i>-values for enrichment and depletion (e.g., fewer common targets than expected), based on the hypergeometric distribution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec18">Network-based separation of drugs</h3><p>A network-based separation of a drug pair, A and B, is calculated via Eq. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#Equ2">2</a>). We evaluated four other different distance measures that take into account the path lengths between two drug target sets: (a) the closest measure, representing the average shortest path length between targets of drug A and the nearest target of the drug A; (b) the shortest measure, representing the average shortest path length among all targets of drugs; (c) the kernel measure, down-weighting longer paths via an exponential penalty; (d) the centre measure, representing the shortest path length among all targets of drugs with the greatest closeness centrality among drug targets. Given A and B, the set of drug targets for A and B, and <i>d</i><sub><i>AB</i></sub>, the shortest path length between nodes a and b in the interactome, we define these distance measures as follows:</p><div id="Equ13" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Closest}}:\langle d_{AB}^C\rangle = \frac{1}{{\left| {\left| A \right|} \right| + \left\Vert B \right\Vert}}\left( {\mathop {\sum }\limits_{a \in A} min_{b \in B}d\left( {a,b} \right) + \mathop {\sum }\limits_{b \in B} min_{a \in A}d\left( {a,b} \right)} \right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (13)
                </div></div><div id="Equ14" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Shortest}}:\langle d_{AB}^S\rangle = \frac{1}{{\left\Vert A \right\Vert \times \left\Vert B \right\Vert}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{a \in A,b \in B} d(a,b)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (14)
                </div></div><div id="Equ15" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Kernel}}:\langle d_{AB}^k\rangle = \frac{{ - 1}}{{\left| {\left| A \right|} \right| + \left\Vert B \right\Vert}}\left( {\mathop {\sum }\limits_{a \in A} {\it{ln}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{b \in B} \frac{{e^{ - (d\left( {a,b} \right) + 1)}}}{{\left\Vert B \right\Vert}} + \mathop {\sum }\limits_{b \in B} {\it{ln}}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{a \in A} \frac{{e^{ - (d\left( {a,b} \right) + 1)}}}{{\left\Vert A \right\Vert}}} \right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (15)
                </div></div><div id="Equ16" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Centre}}:\langle d_{AB}^{cc}\rangle = d(centre_A,centre_B)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (16)
                </div></div><p>where <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_B\)</span>, the topological centre of A, is defined as</p><div id="Equ17" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$centre_B = argmin_{u \in B}\mathop {\sum }\limits_{b \in B} d(b,u)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (17)
                </div></div><p>If the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_A\)</span> or <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_B\)</span> is not unique, all the nodes in <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_A\)</span> or <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_B\)</span> are used to define the centre, and shortest path lengths between these nodes are averaged. If the <span class="mathjax-tex">\(centre_B\)</span> is not unique, all nodes are used to define the centre and the shortest path lengths to these nodes are averaged.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec19">Collecting disease-association genes</h3><p>We integrated disease–gene annotation data from 8 different resources and excluded the duplicated entries (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM1">4</a>). We annotated all protein-coding genes using gene Entrez ID, chromosomal location, and the official gene symbols from the NCBI database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="NCBI Resource Coordinators. Database resources of the National Center for Biotechnology Information. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D7–D19 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4768e4644">55</a></sup>. Each cardiovascular event was defined by MeSH and UMLS vocabularies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bodenreider, O. The Unified Medical Language System (UMLS): integrating biomedical terminology. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D267–D270 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4768e4648">47</a></sup>. In this study, we constructed disease-associated genes for 4 types of cardiovascular events: arrhythmia (MeSH ID: D001145), heart failure (MeSH ID: D006333), myocardial infarction (MeSH ID: D009203), and hypertension/high blood pressure (MeSH ID: D006973).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec20">Performance evaluation</h3><p>We used area under the receiver operating characteristic (ROC) curve (AUC) to evaluate how well the network proximity discriminates FDA-approved or experimentally validated pairwise combinations from random drug pairs. We counted the true positive rate and false positive rate at different network proximities as thresholds to illustrate the ROC curve. As negative drug pairs are not typically reported in the literature or publicly available databases, we use all unknown drug pairs as negative samples. In addition, we selected the same portion of unknown drug pairs as positive samples to control the size imbalance. We repeated this procedure 100 times and reported the average AUC values to compare the performance of different approaches.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec21">Statistical analysis</h3><p>All statistical analyses were performed using the R package (v3.2.3, <a href="http://www.r-project.org/">http://www.r-project.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3" id="Sec22">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM9">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                        

                        <section aria-labelledby="code-availability"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The code for network proximity calculation is available at <a href="http://github.com/emreg00/toolbox">github.com/emreg00/toolbox</a>. All other codes used in this study are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="data-availability"><div class="c-article-section js-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title u-h2 js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content js-collapsible-section" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The publicly available human protein–protein interactome (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM3">1</a>), experimentally validated drug–target interactions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM4">2</a>), experimentally validated drug combinations (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM5">3</a>), clinically reported adverse drug–drug interactions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM6">4</a>), and network-predicted hypertensive drug combinations (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM7">5</a>) are available in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM3">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09186-x#MOESM7">5</a>. The unpublished binary human protein–protein interactions are available at <a href="http://ccsb.dana-farber.org/interactome-data.html">http://ccsb.dana-farber.org/interactome-data.html</a>.</p>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading u-h3">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>A.-L.B. is a co-founder of Scipher, a startup that uses network concepts to explore human disease. The other authors declare no competing interests.</p>
